# Searching Sourcegraph

Search before you build. Existing patterns reduce tokens, ensure consistency, and surface tested solutions.

Use `sg_list_repos` to discover available repositories before searching.


## Tool Selection Logic

**Start here:**

1. **Know the exact symbol or pattern?** → `sg_keyword_search`
2. **Know the concept, not the code?** → `sg_nls_search`
3. **Need to understand how/why?** → `sg_deepsearch_read`
4. **Tracing a symbol's usage?** → `sg_find_references`
5. **Need full implementation?** → `sg_go_to_definition` → `sg_read_file`

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Exact code patterns | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Trace usage | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Understand systems | `sg_deepsearch_read` |
| Read files | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
file:.*\.test\.ts$ -file:__mocks__   # Tests, exclude mocks
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

Combine filters: `repo:^github.com/myorg/backend$ file:src/handlers lang:typescript`

## Context-Aware Behaviour

**When the user provides a file path or error message:**
- Extract symbols, function names, or error codes
- Search for those exact terms first
- Trace references if the error involves a known symbol

**When the user asks "how does X work":**
- Prefer `sg_deepsearch_read` for architectural understanding
- Follow up with `sg_read_file` on key files mentioned in the response

**When the user is implementing a new feature:**
- Search for similar existing implementations first
- Read tests for usage examples
- Check for shared utilities before creating new ones

**When debugging:**
- Search for the exact error string
- Trace the symbol where the error is thrown
- Check recent changes with `sg_diff_search`

## Workflows

For detailed step-by-step workflows, see:
- `workflows/implementing-feature.md` — when building new features
- `workflows/understanding-code.md` — when exploring unfamiliar systems
- `workflows/debugging-issue.md` — when tracking down bugs

## Efficiency Rules

**Minimise tool calls:**
- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms (faster, more precise)

**Batch your understanding:**
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than reading one and asking questions
- Use `sg_deepsearch_read` for "how does X work" instead of multiple keyword searches

**Avoid common token waste:**
- Don't search all repos when you know the target repo
- Don't use `sg_deepsearch_read` for simple "find all" queries
- Don't re-read files you've already seen in this conversation

## Query Patterns

| Intent | Query |
|--------|-------|
| React hooks | `file:.*\.tsx$ use[A-Z].*= \(` |
| API routes | `file:src/api app\.(get\|post\|put\|delete)` |
| Error handling | `catch.*Error\|\.catch\(` |
| Type definitions | `file:types/ export (interface\|type)` |
| Test setup | `file:.*\.test\. beforeEach\|beforeAll` |
| Config files | `file:(webpack\|vite\|rollup)\.config` |
| CI/CD | `file:\.github/workflows deploy` |

For more patterns, see `query-patterns.md`.

## Output Formatting

**Search results:**
- Present as a brief summary, not raw tool output
- Highlight the most relevant file and line
- Include a code snippet only if it directly answers the question

**Code explanations:**
- Start with a one-sentence summary
- Use the codebase's own terminology
- Reference specific files and functions

**Recommendations:**
- Present as numbered steps if actionable
- Link to specific patterns found in the codebase
- Note any existing utilities that should be reused

## Common Mistakes

| Mistake | Fix |
|---------|-----|
| Searching all repos | Add `repo:^github.com/org/repo$` |
| Too many results | Add `file:` pattern or keywords |
| Missing relevant code | Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching |
| Not understanding context | Use `sg_deepsearch_read` |
| Guessing patterns | Read implementations with `sg_read_file` |

## Principles

- Start narrow, expand if needed
- Chain tools: search → read → find references → definition
- Check tests for usage examples
- Read before generating

# Task: Kubernetes client-go Systems Guide

**Repository:** kubernetes/kubernetes (stripped snapshot)
**Output:** Write your document to `/workspace/documentation.md`

## Objective

Produce an advanced systems guide for client-go that explains API access, controller loops, and extension paths.

## Scope

Focus on the client-go subsystem.
Your document must explain component responsibilities, end-to-end flow, and extension or operational tradeoffs.

## Content Expectations

Address all of the following in your own structure:
- subsystem purpose, boundaries, and upstream/downstream dependencies
- key components and how responsibilities are split
- end-to-end control/request flow with concrete repository evidence
- failure modes, limits, and design tradeoffs
- extension points, customization hooks, and associated risks
- a concise map of the most important files/directories used in your analysis

## Quality Bar

- Cite concrete file paths from the repository.
- Explain behavior and interactions, not just API signatures.
- Include at least one section on operational implications (performance, reliability, or maintainability).
- Do not fabricate classes, functions, or files.

## Anti-Requirements

- Do not output `doc.go`.
- Do not provide shallow bullet dumps without system flow explanation.
- Do not rely on external docs not present in the workspace.

{"content":"1: # `client-go` Architecture\n2: \n3: This document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. It describes the\n4: major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\n5: \n6: ## Client Configuration\n7: \n8: There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\n13: \n14: ## REST Client\n15: \n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\n19: \n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\n23: is injected into requests.\n24: \n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\n27: supporting different client models from a common base.\n28: \n29: ### Endpoint Interactions\n30: \n31: *   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\n32:     (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the abili

The document is written to `/workspace/documentation.md`. Here's what it covers and why each section is grounded in real code:

**What the guide contains:**

1. **Subsystem purpose and boundaries** — explains the `v0.X.Y` ↔ `v1.X.Y` versioning contract, upstream/downstream dependency graph, and what client-go explicitly does not own.

2. **Key components and responsibilities** — traces the layered architecture from `rest.Config` (credential sanitization via `fmt.Stringer` in `rest/config.go:196`) through the transport `RoundTripper` chain (`transport/transport.go:36`), the typed `kubernetes.Clientset`, the dynamic client, and the full `tools/cache` stack.

3. **End-to-end flows** — two concrete paths: the simple imperative GET (including 429/retry behavior from `rest/with_retry.go`) and the full informer control loop from `Reflector.ListAndWatchWithContext` → `DeltaFIFO` → `HandleDeltas` → `sharedProcessor` → `processorListener` → `ResourceEventHandler` → work queue → reconcile.

4. **Failure modes and tradeoffs** — covers watch reconnect backoff (800ms–60s exponential), DeltaFIFO deduplication collapse rules, slow-handler backpressure (the informer has no back-pressure; the work queue provides it), the "too old resourceVersion" relist path, and leader election's non-fencing guarantee with clock-skew tolerance.

5. **Extension points with risks** — `WrapTransport`, `SetTransform`, `WithTweakListOptions`, `AddIndexers`, `SetWatchErrorHandlerWithContext`, and SSA via `applyconfigurations`, each with documented pre-conditions and failure modes.

6. **Operational implications** — memory footprint management via `TransformFunc`, the `sharedInformerFactory` deduplication by `reflect.Type`, work queue rate limiter composition (per-item exponential + global token bucket), featu

8. mcp__sourcegraph__sg_read_file @ -
Input
{
  "path": "staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md",
  "repo": "github.com/sg-benchmarks/kubernetes--stripped"
}
Output
{
  "content": "{\"content\":\"1: # `client-go` Architecture\\n2: \\n3: This document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. It describes the\\n4: major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\\n5: \\n6: ## Client Configuration\\n7: \\n8: There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\\n13: \\n14: ## REST Client\\n15: \\n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\\n19: \\n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\\n23: is injected into requests.\\n24: \\n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\\n27: supporting different client models from a common base.\\n28: \\n29: ### Endpoint Interactions\\n30: \\n31: *   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\\n32:     (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\\n33:     request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\\n34:     Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \\n35: *   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\\n36:     object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\\n37:     `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\\n38: *   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\\n39:     return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\\n40:     responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\\n41:     Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\\n42: *   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\\n43:     `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\\n44:     objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\\n45:     the underlying streaming protocol.\\n46: \\n47: ### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\\n48: \\n49: *   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\\n50:     `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\\n51: *   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\\n52:     `WarningHandler`.\\n53: *   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\\n54:     client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\\n55: *   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\\n56:     responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\\n57: \\n58: ## Typed and Dynamic Clients\\n59: \\n60: To handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\\n61: models.\\n62: \\n63: The **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\\n64: \\n65: The **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\\n66: to interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\\n67: 1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\\n68:     **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\\n69: 2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\\n70:     resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\\n71: \\n72: ## Code Generation\\n73: \\n74: A core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\\n75: strongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\\n76: controller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\\n77: several key components:\\n78: \\n79: *   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\\n80: *   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\\n81: *   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\\n82: *   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\\n83: \\n84: A contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\\n85: of these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\\n86: \\n87: `sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\\n88: \\n89: ## Controller Infrastructure\\n90: \\n91: The `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\\n92: request-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\\n93: \\n94: The data flow is as follows:\\n95: \\n96: ```mermaid\\n97: graph TD\\n98:     subgraph \\\"Kubernetes API\\\"\\n99:         API_Server[API Server]\\n100:     end\\n101: \\n102:     subgraph \\\"client-go: Informer Mechanism\\\"\\n103:         Reflector(\\\"1. Reflector\\\")\\n104:         DeltaFIFO(\\\"2. DeltaFIFO\\\")\\n105:         Indexer[\\\"3. Indexer (Cache)\\\"]\\n106:         EventHandlers(\\\"4. Event Handlers\\\")\\n107:     end\\n108: \\n109:     subgraph \\\"User Code\\\"\\n110:         WorkQueue[\\\"5. Work Queue\\\"]\\n111:         Controller(\\\"6. Controller\\\")\\n112:     end\\n113: \\n114:     API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\\n115:     Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\\n116:     DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\\n117:     Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\\n118:     EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\\n119:     WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\\n120:     Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\\n121: ```\\n122: \\n123: A **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\\n124: `resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\\n125: stream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\\n126: **\\\"too old\\\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\\n127: `Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\\n128: `resourceVersion` to restart from.\\n129: \\n130: The **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\\n131: logic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\\n132: \\n133: ## Controller Patterns\\n134: \\n135: The controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\\n136: ensure resiliency.\\n137: \\n138: The **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\\n139: and reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\\n140: work queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\\n141: blocking the informer's watch stream.\\n142: \\n143: For high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\\n144: solution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\\n145: shared `Lease` object.\\n146: \\n147: ## Server-Side Apply\\n148: \\n149: `client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\\n150: server's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\\n151: model that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\\n152: **`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\\n153: construct the declarative patch.\\n154: \\n155: ## Versioning and Compatibility\\n156: \\n157: `client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\\n158: version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\\n159: \\n160: The Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\\n161: version of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\\n162: A contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\\n163: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-benchmarks/kubernetes--stripped@6d5678ae074d17945f174da2b064603131370a5f/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md\"}",
  "structuredContent": {
    "content": "1: # `client-go` Architecture\n2: \n3: This document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. It describes the\n4: major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\n5: \n6: ## Client Configuration\n7: \n8: There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\n13: \n14: ## REST Client\n15: \n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\n19: \n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\n23: is injected into requests.\n24: \n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\n27: supporting different client models from a common base.\n28: \n29: ### Endpoint Interactions\n30: \n31: *   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\n32:     (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\n33:     request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\n34:     Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \n35: *   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\n36:     object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\n37:     `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\n38: *   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\n39:     return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\n40:     responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\n41:     Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\n42: *   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\n43:     `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\n44:     objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\n45:     the underlying streaming protocol.\n46: \n47: ### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\n48: \n49: *   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\n50:     `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\n51: *   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\n52:     `WarningHandler`.\n53: *   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\n54:     client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\n55: *   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\n56:     responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\n57: \n58: ## Typed and Dynamic Clients\n59: \n60: To handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\n61: models.\n62: \n63: The **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\n64: \n65: The **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\n66: to interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\n67: 1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\n68:     **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\n69: 2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\n70:     resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\n71: \n72: ## Code Generation\n73: \n74: A core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\n75: strongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\n76: controller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\n77: several key components:\n78: \n79: *   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\n80: *   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\n81: *   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\n82: *   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\n83: \n84: A contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\n85: of these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\n86: \n87: `sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\n88: \n89: ## Controller Infrastructure\n90: \n91: The `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\n92: request-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\n93: \n94: The data flow is as follows:\n95: \n96: ```mermaid\n97: graph TD\n98:     subgraph \"Kubernetes API\"\n99:         API_Server[API Server]\n100:     end\n101: \n102:     subgraph \"client-go: Informer Mechanism\"\n103:         Reflector(\"1. Reflector\")\n104:         DeltaFIFO(\"2. DeltaFIFO\")\n105:         Indexer[\"3. Indexer (Cache)\"]\n106:         EventHandlers(\"4. Event Handlers\")\n107:     end\n108: \n109:     subgraph \"User Code\"\n110:         WorkQueue[\"5. Work Queue\"]\n111:         Controller(\"6. Controller\")\n112:     end\n113: \n114:     API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\n115:     Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\n116:     DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\n117:     Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\n118:     EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\n119:     WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\n120:     Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\n121: ```\n122: \n123: A **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\n124: `resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\n125: stream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\n126: **\"too old\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\n127: `Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\n128: `resourceVersion` to restart from.\n129: \n130: The **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\n131: logic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\n132: \n133: ## Controller Patterns\n134: \n135: The controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\n136: ensure resiliency.\n137: \n138: The **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\n139: and reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\n140: work queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\n141: blocking the informer's watch stream.\n142: \n143: For high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\n144: solution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\n145: shared `Lease` object.\n146: \n147: ## Server-Side Apply\n148: \n149: `client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\n150: server's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\n151: model that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\n152: **`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\n153: construct the declarative patch.\n154: \n155: ## Versioning and Compatibility\n156: \n157: `client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\n158: version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\n159: \n160: The Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\n161: version of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\n162: A contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\n163: ",

An object is identified by its API group, kind/resource,\\n43: // namespace (if any), and name; the `ObjectMeta.UID` is not part of\\n44: // an object's ID as far as this contract is concerned.  One\\n45: // SharedInformer provides linkage to objects of a particular API\\n46: // group and kind/resource.  The linked object collection of a\\n47: // SharedInformer may be further restricted to one namespace (if\\n48: // applicable) and/or by label selector and/or field selector.\\n49: //\\n50: // The authoritative state of an object is what apiservers provide\\n51: // access to, and an object goes through a strict sequence of states.\\n52: // An object state is either (1) present with a ResourceVersion and\\n53: // other appropriate content or (2) \\\"absent\\\".\\n54: //\\n55: // A SharedInformer maintains a local cache --- exposed by GetStore(),\\n56: // by GetIndexer() in the case of an indexed informer, and possibly by\\n57: // machinery involved in creating and/or accessing the informer --- of\\n58: // the state of each relevant object.  This cache is eventually\\n59: // consistent with the authoritative state.  This means that, unless\\n60: // prevented by persistent communication problems, if ever a\\n61: // particular object ID X is authoritatively associated with a state S\\n62: // then for every SharedInformer I whose collection includes (X, S)\\n63: // eventually either (1) I's cache associates X with S or a later\\n64: // state of X, (2) I is stopped, or (3) the authoritative state\\n65: // service for X terminates.  To be formally complete, we say that the\\n66: // absent state meets any restriction by label selector or field\\n67: // selector.\\n68: //\\n69: // For a given informer and relevant object ID X, the sequence of\\n70: // states that appears in the informer's cache is a subsequence of the\\n71: // states authoritatively associated with X.  That is, some states\\n72: // might never appear in the cache but ordering among the appearing\\n73: // states is correct.  Note, however, that there is no promise about\\n74: // ordering between states seen for different objects.\\n75: //\\n76: // The local cache starts out empty, and gets populated and updated\\n77: // during `Run()`.\\n78: //\\n79: // As a simple example, if a collection of objects is henceforth\\n80: // unchanging, a SharedInformer is created that links to that\\n81: // collection, and that SharedInformer is `Run()` then that\\n82: // SharedInformer's cache eventually holds an exact copy of that\\n83: // collection (unless it is stopped too soon, the authoritative state\\n84: // service ends, or communication problems between the two\\n85: // persistently thwart achievement).\\n86: //\\n87: // As another simple example, if the local cache ever holds a\\n88: // non-absent state for some object ID and the object is eventually\\n89: // removed from the authoritative state then eventually the object is\\n90: // removed from the local cache (unless the SharedInformer is stopped\\n91: // too soon, the authoritative state service ends, or communication\\n92: // problems persistently thwart the desired result).\\n93: //\\n94: // The keys in the Store are of the form namespace/name for namespaced\\n95: // objects, and are simply the name for non-namespaced objects.\\n96: // Clients can use `MetaNamespaceKeyFunc(obj)` to extract the key for\\n97: // a given object, and `SplitMetaNamespaceKey(key)` to split a key\\n98: // into its constituent parts.\\n99: //\\n100: // Every query against the local cache is answered entirely from one\\n101: // snapshot of the cache's state.  Thus, the result of a `List` call\\n102: // will not contain two entries with the same namespace and name.\\n103: //\\n104: // A client is identified here by a ResourceEventHandler.  For every\\n105: // update to the SharedInformer's local cache and for every client\\n106: // added before `Run()`, eventually either the SharedInformer is\\n107: // stopped or the client is notified of the update.  A client added\\n108: // after `Run()` starts gets a startup batch of notifications of\\n109: // additions of the objects existing in the cache at the time that\\n110: // client was added; also, for every update to the SharedInformer's\\n111: // local cache after that client was added, eventually either the\\n112: // SharedInformer is stopped or that client is notified of that\\n113: // update.  Client notifications happen after the corresponding cache\\n114: // update and, in the case of a SharedIndexInformer, after the\\n115: // corresponding index updates.  It is possible that additional cache\\n116: // and index updates happen before such a prescribed notification.\\n117: // For a given SharedInformer and client, the notifications are\\n118: // delivered sequentially.  For a given SharedInformer, client, and\\n119: // object ID, the notifications are delivered in order.  Because\\n120: // `ObjectMeta.UID` has no role in identifying objects, it is possible\\n121: // that when (1) object O1 with ID (e.g. namespace and name) X and\\n122: // `ObjectMeta.UID` U1 in the SharedInformer's local cache is deleted\\n123: // and later (2) another object O2 with ID X and ObjectMeta.UID U2 is\\n124: // created the informer's clients are not notified of (1) and (2) but\\n125: // rather are notified only of an update from O1 to O2. Clients that\\n126: // need to detect such cases might do so by comparing the `ObjectMeta.UID`\\n127: // field of the old and the new object in the code that handles update\\n128: // notifications (i.e. `OnUpdate` method of ResourceEventHandler).\\n129: //\\n130: // A client must process each notification promptly; a SharedInformer\\n131: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-benchmarks/kubernetes--stripped@6d5678ae074d17945f174da2b064603131370a5f/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/shared_informer.go%3FL1-130\"}",

    "content": "1: /*\n2: Copyright 2015 The Kubernetes Authors.\n3: \n4: Licensed under the Apache License, Version 2.0 (the \"License\");\n5: you may not use this file except in compliance with the License.\n6: You may obtain a copy of the License at\n7: \n8:     http://www.apache.org/licenses/LICENSE-2.0\n9: \n10: Unless required by applicable law or agreed to in writing, software\n11: distributed under the License is distributed on an \"AS IS\" BASIS,\n12: WITHOUT WARRANTIES OR CONDITIONS OF ANY KIND, either express or implied.\n13: See the License for the specific language governing permissions and\n14: limitations under the License.\n15: */\n16: \n17: package cache\n18: \n19: import (\n20: \t\"context\"\n21: \t\"errors\"\n22: \t\"fmt\"\n23: \t\"sync\"\n24: \t\"time\"\n25: \n26: \t\"k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/api/meta\"\n27: \t\"k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/runtime\"\n28: \tutilruntime \"k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/util/runtime\"\n29: \t\"k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/util/wait\"\n30: \t\"k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/synctrack\"\n31: \t\"k8s.io/utils/buffer\"\n32: \t\"k8s.io/utils/clock\"\n33: \t\"k8s.io/utils/ptr\"\n34: \n35: \t\"k8s.io/klog/v2\"\n36: \n37: \tclientgofeaturegate \"k8s.io/client-go/features\"\n38: )\n39: \n40: // SharedInformer provides eventually consistent linkage of its\n41: // clients to the authoritative state of a given collection of\n42: // objects.  An object is identified by its API group, kind/resource,\n43: // namespace (if any), and name; the `ObjectMeta.UID` is not part of\n44: // an object's ID as far as this contract is concerned.  One\n45: // SharedInformer provides linkage to objects of a particular API\n46: // group and kind/resource.  The linked object collection of a\n47: // SharedInformer may be further restricted to one namespace (if\n48: // applicable) and/or by label selector and/or field selector.\n49: //\n50: // The authoritative state of an object is what apiservers provide\n51: // access to, and an object goes through a strict sequence of states.\n52: // An object state is either (1) present with a ResourceVersion and\n53: // other appropriate content or (2) \"absent\".\n54: //\n55: // A SharedInformer maintains a local cache --- exposed by GetStore(),\n56: // by GetIndexer() in the case of an indexed informer, and possibly by\n57: // machinery involved in creating and/or accessing the informer --- of\n58: // the state of each relevant object.  This cache is eventually\n59: // consistent with the authoritative state.  This means that, unless\n60: // prevented by persistent communication problems, if ever a\n61: // particular object ID X is authoritatively associated with a state S\n62: // then for every SharedInformer I whose collection includes (X, S)\n63: // eventually either (1) I's cache associates X with S or a later\n64: // state of X, (2) I is stopped, or (3) the authoritative state\n65: // service for X terminates.  To be formally complete, we say that the\n66: // absent state meets any restriction by label selector or field\n67: // selector.\n68: //\n69: // For a given informer and relevant object ID X, the sequence of\n70: // states that appears in the informer's cache is a subsequence of the\n71: // states authoritatively associated with X.  That is, some states\n72: // might never appear in the cache but ordering among the appearing\n73: // states is correct.  Note, however, that there is no promise about\n74: // ordering between states seen for different objects.\n75: //\n76: // The local cache starts out empty, and gets populated and updated\n77: // during `Run()`.\n78: //\n79: // As a simple example, if a collection of objects is henceforth\n80: // unchanging, a SharedInformer is created that links to that\n81: // collection, and that SharedInformer is `Run()` then that\n82: // SharedInformer's cache eventually holds an exact copy of that\n83: // collection (unless it is stopped too soon, the authoritative state\n84: // service ends, or communication problems between the two\n85: // persistently thwart achievement).\n86: //\n87: // As another simple example, if the local cache ever holds a\n88: // non-absent state for some object ID and the object is eventually\n89: // removed from the authoritative state then eventually the object is\n90: // removed from the local cache (unless the SharedInformer is stopped\n91: // too soon, the authoritative state service ends, or communication\n92: // problems persistently thwart the desired result).\n93: //\n94: // The keys in the Store are of the form namespace/name for namespaced\n95: // objects, and are simply the name for non-namespaced objects.\n96: // Clients can use `MetaNamespaceKeyFunc(obj)` to extract the key for\n97: // a given object, and `SplitMetaNamespaceKey(key)` to split a key\n98: // into its constituent parts.\n99: //\n100: // Every query against the local cache is answered entirely from one\n101: // snapshot of the cache's state.  Thus, the result of a `List` call\n102: // will not contain two entries with the same namespace and name.\n103: //\n104: // A client is identified here by a ResourceEventHandler.  For every\n105: // update to the SharedInformer's local cache and for every client\n106: // added before `Run()`, eventually either the SharedInformer is\n107: // stopped or the client is notified of the update.  A client added\n108: // after `Run()` starts gets a startup batch of notifications of\n109: // additions of the objects existing in the cache at the time that\n110: // client was added; also, for every update to the SharedInformer's\n111: // local cache after that client was added, eventually either the\n112: // SharedInformer is stopped or that client is notified of that\n113: // update.  Client notifications happen after the corresponding cache\n114: // update and, in the case of a SharedIndexInformer, after the\n115: // corresponding index updates.  It is possible that additional cache\n116: // and index updates happen before such a prescribed notification.\n117: // For a given SharedInformer and client, the notifications are\n118: // delivered sequentially.  For a given SharedInformer, client, and\n119: // object ID, the notifications are delivered in order.  Because\n120: // `ObjectMeta.UID` has no role in identifying objects, it is possible\n121: // that when (1) object O1 with ID (e.g. namespace and name) X and\n122: // `ObjectMeta.UID` U1 in the SharedInformer's local cache is deleted\n123: // and later (2) another object O2 with ID X and ObjectMeta.UID U2 is\n124: // created the informer's clients are not notified of (1) and (2) but\n125: // rather are notified only of an update from O1 to O2. Clients that\n126: // need to detect such cases might do so by comparing the `ObjectMeta.UID`\n127: // field of the old and the new object in the code that handles update\n128: // notifications (i.e. `OnUpdate` method of ResourceEventHandler).\n129: //\n130: // A client must process each notification promptly; a SharedInformer\n131: ",

The error returned in 'err' must satisfy the\n93: \t// following conditions:\n94: \t//  a: errors.Unwrap(err) = errors.Unwrap(finalErr) if finalErr\n95: \t//     implements Unwrap\n96: \t//  b: errors.Unwrap(err) = finalErr if finalErr does not\n97: \t//     implements Unwrap\n98: \t//  c: errors.Is(err, otherErr) = errors.Is(finalErr, otherErr)\n99: \tWrapPreviousError(finalErr error) (err error)\n100: }\n101: \n102: // RetryAfter holds information associated with the next retry.\n103: type RetryAfter struct {\n104: \t// Wait is the duration the server has asked us to wait before\n105: \t// the next retry is initiated.\n106: \t// This is the value of the 'Retry-After' response header in seconds.\n107: \tWait time.Duration\n108: \n109: \t// Attempt is the Nth attempt after which we have received a retryable\n110: \t// error or a 'Retry-After' response header from the server.\n111: \tAttempt int\n112: \n113: \t// Reason describes why we are retrying the request\n114: \tReason string\n115: }\n116: \n117: type withRetry struct {\n118: \tmaxRetries int\n119: \tattempts   int\n120: \n121: ",

    "content": "1: /*\n2: Copyright 2015 The Kubernetes Authors.\n3: \n4: Licensed under the Apache License, Version 2.0 (the \"License\");\n5: you may not use this file except in compliance with the License.\n6: You may obtain a copy of the License at\n7: \n8:     http://www.apache.org/licenses/LICENSE-2.0\n9: \n10: Unless required by applicable law or agreed to in writing, software\n11: distributed under the License is distributed on an \"AS IS\" BASIS,\n12: WITHOUT WARRANTIES OR CONDITIONS OF ANY KIND, either express or implied.\n13: See the License for the specific language governing permissions and\n14: limitations under the License.\n15: */\n16: \n17: // Package leaderelection implements leader election of a set of endpoints.\n18: // It uses an annotation in the endpoints object to store the record of the\n19: // election state. This implementation does not guarantee that only one\n20: // client is acting as a leader (a.k.a. fencing).\n21: //\n22: // A client only acts on timestamps captured locally to infer the state of the\n23: // leader election. The client does not consider timestamps in the leader\n24: // election record to be accurate because these timestamps may not have been\n25: // produced by a local clock. The implemention does not depend on their\n26: // accuracy and only uses their change to indicate that another client has\n27: // renewed the leader lease. Thus the implementation is tolerant to arbitrary\n28: // clock skew, but is not tolerant to arbitrary clock skew rate.\n29: //\n30: // However the level of tolerance to skew rate can be configured by setting\n31: // RenewDeadline and LeaseDuration appropriately. The tolerance expressed as a\n32: // maximum tolerated ratio of time passed on the fastest node to time passed on\n33: // the slowest node can be approximately achieved with a configuration that sets\n34: // the same ratio of LeaseDuration to RenewDeadline. For example if a user wanted\n35: // to tolerate some nodes progressing forward in time twice as fast as other nodes,\n36: // the user could set LeaseDuration to 60 seconds and RenewDeadline to 30 seconds.\n37: //\n38: // While not required, some method of clock synchronization between nodes in the\n39: // cluster is highly recommended. It's important to keep in mind when configuring\n40: // this client that the tolerance to skew rate varies inversely to master\n41: // availability.\n42: //\n43: // Larger clusters often have a more lenient SLA for API latency. This should be\n44: // taken into account when configuring the client. The rate of leader transitions\n45: // should be monitored and RetryPeriod and LeaseDuration should be increased\n46: // until the rate is stable and acceptably low. It's important to keep in mind\n47: // when configuring this client that the tolerance to API latency varies inversely\n48: // to master availability.\n49: //\n50: // DISCLAIMER: this is an alpha API. ok {\n721: \t\treturn processDeltas(s, s.indexer, deltas, isInInitialList)\n722: \t}\n723: \treturn errors.New(\"object given as Process argument is not Deltas\")\n724: }\n725: \n726: func (s *sharedIndexInformer) HandleBatchDeltas(deltas []Delta, isInInitialList bool) error {\n727: \ts.blockDeltas.Lock()\n728: \tdefer s.blockDeltas.Unlock()\n729: \treturn processDeltasInBatch(s, s.indexer, deltas, isInInitialList)\n730: }\n731: \n732: // Conforms to ResourceEventHandler\n733: func (s *sharedIndexInformer) OnAdd(obj interface{}, isInInitialList bool) {\n734: \t// Invocation of this function is locked under s.blockDeltas, so it is\n735: \t// safe to distribute the notification\n736: \ts.cacheMutationDetector.AddObject(obj)\n737: \ts.processor.distribute(addNotification{newObj: obj, isInInitialList: isInInitialList}, false)\n738: }\n739: \n740: // Conforms to ResourceEventHandler\n741: func (s *sharedIndexInformer) OnUpdate(old, new interface{}) {\n742: \tisSync := false\n743: \n744: \t// If is a Sync event, isSync should be true\n745: \t// If is a Replaced event, isSync is true if resource version is unchanged.\n746: \t// If RV is unchanged: this is a Sync/Replaced event, so isSync is true\n747: \n748: \tif accessor, err := meta.Accessor(new); err == nil {\n749: \t\tif oldAccessor, err := meta.Accessor(old); err == nil {\n750: \t\t\t// Events that didn't change resourceVersion are treated as resync events\n751: \t\t\t// and only propagated to listeners that requested resync\n752: \t\t\tisSync = accessor.GetResourceVersion() == oldAccessor.GetResourceVersion()\n753: \t\t}\n754: \t}\n755: \n756: \t// Invocation of this function is locked under s.blockDeltas, so it is\n757: \t// safe to distribute the notification\n758: \ts.cacheMutationDetector.AddObject(new)\n759: \ts.processor.distribute(updateNotification{oldObj: old, newObj: new}, isSync)\n760: }\n761: \n762: // Conforms to ResourceEventHandler\n763: func (s *sharedIndexInformer) OnDelete(old interface{}) {\n764: \t// Invocation of this function is locked under s.blockDeltas, so it is\n765: \t// safe to distribute the notification\n766: \ts.processor.distribute(deleteNotification{oldObj: old}, false)\n767: }\n768: \n769: // IsStopped reports whether the informer has already been stopped\n770: func (s *sharedIndexInformer) IsStopped() bool {\n771: \ts.startedLock.Lock()\n772: \tdefer s.startedLock.Unlock()\n773: \treturn s.stopped\n774: }\n775: \n776: func (s *sharedIndexInformer) RemoveEventHandler(handle ResourceEventHandlerRegistration) error {\n777: \ts.startedLock.Lock()\n778: \tdefer s.startedLock.Unlock()\n779: \n780: \t// in order to safely remove, we have to\n781: \t// 1. stop sending add/update/delete notifications\n782: \t// 2. remove and stop listener\n783: \t// 3. unblock\n784: \ts.blockDeltas.Lock()\n785: \tdefer s.blockDeltas.Unlock()\n786: \treturn s.processor.removeListener(handle)\n787: }\n788: \n789: // sharedProcessor has a collection of processorListener and can\n790: // distribute a notification object to its listeners.  There are two\n791: // kinds of distribute operations.  The sync distributions go to a\n792: // subset of the listeners that (a) is recomputed in the occasional\n793: // calls to shouldResync and (b) every listener is initially put in.\n794: // The non-sync distributions go to every listener.\n795: type sharedProcessor struct {\n796: \tlistenersStarted bool\n797: \tlistenersLock    sync.RWMutex\n798: \t// Map from listeners to whether or not they are currently syncing\n799: \tlisteners map[*processorListener]bool\n800: \tclock     clock.Clock\n801: \twg        wait.Group\n802: }\n803: \n804: func (p *sharedProcessor) getListener(registration ResourceEventHandlerRegistration) *processorListener {\n805: \tp.listenersLock.RLock()\n806: \tdefer p.listenersLock.RUnlock()\n807: \n808: \tif p.listeners == nil {\n809: \t\treturn nil\n810: \t}\n811: \n812: \tif result, ok := registration.(*processorListener);   "content": "# client-go: Advanced Systems Guide\n\n## 1. Subsystem Purpose, Boundaries, and Dependencies\n\n`client-go` is the official Go client library for the Kubernetes API. It occupies a precise position in the dependency graph: it sits **above** `apimachinery` (which owns the type system and serialization) and **below** every Kubernetes controller, operator, and CLI tool that needs to talk to the API server.\n\nIts canonical source lives in the Kubernetes monorepo under `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/` and is published as a standalone module at `k8s.io/client-go`. A `client-go` release `v0.X.Y` tracks Kubernetes `v1.X.Y` exactly; a client built against an older version will work against a newer server, but not the reverse (the official compatibility guarantee flows one direction only).\n\n**Upstream dependencies** (things client-go consumes):\n- `k8s.io/apimachinery` \u2014 `runtime.Object`, `schema.GroupVersionKind`, `watch.Event`, `meta.Accessor`, all error helpers (`apierrors.IsNotFound`, etc.).\n- Standard Go TLS and HTTP stacks (`net/http`, `crypto/tls`).\n- `golang.org/x/time/rate` for token-bucket rate limiting in the work queue.\n\n**Downstream dependents** (things that consume client-go):\n- Every built-in controller in `k8s.io/kubernetes` (the scheduler, the replication controller, the deployment controller, etc.).\n- Operator SDK, controller-runtime, kubebuilder, Argo, Flux \u2014 all built on the informer/work-queue pattern exposed here.\n- `kubectl` and every other CLI tool that speaks to the API server.\n\n**What client-go does NOT own:**\n- API type definitions (owned by `k8s.io/api`).\n- API server implementation (owned by `k8s.io/apiserver`).\n- Code generation tooling (owned by `k8s.io/code-generator`).\n\n---\n\n## 2. Key Components and How Responsibilities Are Split\n\n### 2.1 Configuration Layer\n\n`rest.Config` (`staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/rest/config.go`) is the in-memory representation of everything needed to connect to an API server. It carries transport-level concerns (TLS certificates, proxy, dial function), auth credentials (bearer token, exec-plugin config, OIDC auth provider), and policy (QPS, burst, timeout, warning handler).\n\nThere are two standard factories for `rest.Config`:\n\n- **`rest.InClusterConfig()`** (`rest/config.go:543`) reads the pod's projected service account token from `/var/run/secrets/kubernetes.io/serviceaccount/token` and the cluster CA from the same directory, using the `KUBERNETES_SERVICE_HOST`/`KUBERNETES_SERVICE_PORT` environment variables injected by the kubelet.\n- **`tools/clientcmd`** handles the full kubeconfig file parsing, context merging, and external auth provider delegation for out-of-cluster use.\n\nThe separation is deliberate: loading a config is one concern; using it is another. The `rest.Config` struct implements `fmt.Stringer` with automatic redaction of sensitive fields (`BearerToken`, `Password`, `KeyData`) to prevent credential leakage through log output.\n\n### 2.2 REST Client Layer\n\n`rest.RESTClient` (`staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/rest/client.go`) is the foundational HTTP client. It provides a builder API (`Verb()`, `Resource()`, `Namespace()`, `Name()`, `SubResource()`, `Body()`) that constructs a `rest.Request` object (`rest/request.go`). Execution is deferred until a terminal method \u2014 `Do()`, `DoRaw()`, `Stream()`, or `Watch()` \u2014 is called.\n\nThe `Request` struct holds both the request construction state and the execution policy:\n\n```go\n// staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/rest/request.go:96\ntype Request struct {\n    c          *RESTClient\n    rateLimiter flowcontrol.RateLimiter\n    backoff     BackoffManagerWithContext\n    timeout     time.Duration\n    maxRetries  int\n    verb        string\n    resource    string\n    resourceName string\n    // ...\n}\n```\n\nThe transport layer (`staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/transport/transport.go`) assembles a chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects via `transport.New()`. Each link in the chain handles one concern: TLS configuration, adding the `Authorization` header (bearer token or exec-plugin token), impersonation headers, user-agent, etc. This is the mechanism for **all** authentication \u2014 it is injected at transport construction time, not at request time. Callers use `rest.Config.WrapTransport` to inject additional middleware into this chain.\n\nContent negotiation happens via the `Accept` header. Clients can request:\n- `application/json` or `application/vnd.kubernetes.protobuf` for full objects.\n- `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` for metadata-only responses (a major memory optimization for large lists).\n- `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` for tabular output (used by `kubectl get`).\n\n### 2.3 Typed Clientset\n\n`kubernetes.Clientset` (`staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/kubernetes/`) is a generated facade over `rest.RESTClient`. It provides a compile-time-typed, fluent interface for every built-in API group:\n\n```go\npod, err := clientset.CoreV1().Pods(\"default\").Get(ctx, \"my-pod\", metav1.GetOptions{})\n```\n\nEach API group method (e.g., `CoreV1()`, `AppsV1()`) returns a group interface that exposes a `RESTClient()` method for callers who need to escape to the raw level. The clientset is constructed from a single `rest.Config` via `kubernetes.NewForConfig()`.\n\n### 2.4 Dynamic and Discovery Clients\n\n`dynamic.DynamicClient` (`staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/dynamic/`) represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, bypassing the typed code-generation layer. This is the entry point for CRD-backed resources and is required when the resource type is not known at compile time.\n\n`discovery.DiscoveryClient` (`staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/discovery/`) queries the API server's discovery endpoints (`/api`, `/apis`) to determine what resource types exist and what verbs they support. The `CachedDiscoveryClient` wraps it with on-disk caching (defaulting to `~/.kube/cache/discovery/`) to avoid hammering the API server on every invocation.\n\n### 2.5 Controller Infrastructure (tools/cache)\n\nThis is the most architecturally significant part of client-go. The `tools/cache` package (`staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/`) implements a high-throughput, low-overhead event-driven caching system that underpins every Kubernetes controller.\n\nKey types:\n\n| Type | File | Role |\n|---|---|---|\n| `Reflector` | `reflector.go` | Drives LIST+WATCH against the API server; feeds the Store |\n| `DeltaFIFO` | `delta_fifo.go` | Accumulates change events per object key, with deduplication |\n| `sharedIndexInformer` | `shared_informer.go` | Coordinates Reflector, DeltaFIFO, Indexer, and fan-out to listeners |\n| `Indexer` | `index.go` | Thread-safe local cache with secondary index support |\n| `sharedProcessor` | `shared_informer.go` | Fan-out delivery of notifications to registered handlers |\n\n### 2.6 Work Queue\n\n`util/workqueue` (`staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/util/workqueue/`) provides the deduplicating, rate-limited work queue that forms the boundary between the informer machinery and controller business logic.\n\n### 2.7 Leader Election\n\n`tools/leaderelection` (`staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/leaderelection/leaderelection.go`) implements lease-based leader election using Kubernetes `Lease` objects or other `ResourceLock` implementations, ensuring single-writer semantics for HA controllers.\n\n---\n\n## 3. End-to-End Request and Control Flow\n\n### 3.1 Simple Imperative Request\n\n```\nApplication code\n  \u2192 clientset.CoreV1().Pods(\"ns\").Get(ctx, \"name\", opts)\n  \u2192 rest.Request.Do(ctx)           [request.go]\n  \u2192 rateLimiter.Wait(ctx)          [token bucket, flowcontrol/]\n  \u2192 http.RoundTripper chain        [transport/transport.go]\n      \u2192 auth roundtripper (injects Authorization header)\n      \u2192 TLS transport (mTLS to apiserver)\n  \u2192 API server (HTTPS)\n  \u2192 response deserialized via NegotiatedSerializer\n  \u2192 *v1.Pod returned to caller\n```\n\nIf the API server responds with HTTP 429, the `withRetry` middleware (`rest/with_retry.go`) reads the `Retry-After` header and waits before retrying (up to `maxRetries` times). Responses with non-2xx status codes are deserialized into `*apierrors.StatusError`, enabling structured checks like `apierrors.IsNotFound(err)`.\n\n### 3.2 Informer Control Loop (Event-Driven Path)\n\nThis is the critical path for all production controllers. The full data flow is:\n\n```\n[API Server]\n    |\n    | LIST (initial, paginated) then WATCH (streaming)\n    v\n[Reflector] \u2014 staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/reflector.go\n    |\n    | Calls store.Replace() on initial list, then Add/Update/Delete on watch events\n    v\n[DeltaFIFO] \u2014 staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/delta_fifo.go\n    |  key \u2192 []Delta (Add, Update, Delete, Replace, Sync)\n    |\n    | controller.processLoop() pops from queue (holding DeltaFIFO lock)\n    v\n[sharedIndexInformer.HandleDeltas()] \u2014 shared_informer.go:716\n    |\n    | processDeltas(): for each Delta:\n    |   1. Updates the Indexer (in-memory cache + secondary indexes)\n    |   2. Calls sharedProcessor.distribute()\n    v\n[sharedProcessor] \u2014 shared_informer.go:795\n    |  Fan-out: for each registered processorListener\n    v\n[processorListener.add()] \u2014 queues notification to per-listener channel\n    |\n    | processorListener.pop() goroutine drains addCh \u2192 nextCh\n    | processorListener.run() goroutine drains nextCh, calls handler methods\n    v\n[ResourceEventHandler.OnAdd / OnUpdate / OnDelete]\n    |  (user code \u2014 informer event handler)\n    |  Should be fast; enqueue key to work queue, do NOT reconcile here\n    v\n[workqueue.TypedRateLimitingInterface] \u2014 util/workqueue/\n    |\n    | Worker goroutines call queue.Get(), reconcile, queue.Done()\n    | On failure: queue.AddRateLimited() with exponential backoff\n    v\n[Controller business logic]\n    |  Reads from Indexer/Lister (cache) via:\n    |  lister.Pods(\"ns\").Get(\"name\") \u2014 no API call\n    |  Writes mutations to API server via clientset\n    v\n[API Server]\n```\n\nThe `Reflector.ListAndWatchWithContext()` (`reflector.go:408`) governs the initial sync:\n1. If `WatchListClient` feature gate is enabled, it tries the more efficient streaming `watchList` path first.\n2. Otherwise (or on fallback), it calls `r.list()` which issues a paginated LIST using `tools/pager` to handle continuation tokens automatically.\n3. After the initial list, `r.watchWithResync()` starts a background goroutine for periodic resync and enters the main watch loop in `r.watch()`.\n\nThe watch loop requests bookmarks (`AllowWatchBookmarks: true`) from the API server. Bookmarks are synthetic watch events that carry an updated `resourceVersion` without representing an object change. They allow the Reflector to track progress without receiving a real event, which is critical for efficient recovery from watch restarts.\n\n**Recovery from \"too old resource version\":** When the Reflector receives an `isExpiredError`, it sets `isLastSyncResourceVersionUnavailable = true` and re-lists from scratch (resource version `\"\"`), getting a fresh consistent snapshot. Watch bookmarks reduce the frequency of this expensive operation by keeping `lastSyncResourceVersion` more current.\n\n---\n\n## 4. Failure Modes, Limits, and Design Tradeoffs\n\n### 4.1 Watch Connection Drops\n\nThe Reflector's `watch()` loop (`reflector.go:499`) is designed to be resilient. On connection drop, it catches the error, calls `watchErrorHandler`, and uses an `ExponentialBackoffManager` initialized with:\n\n```go\n// reflector.go:282\nbackoffManager: wait.NewExponentialBackoffManager(\n    800*time.Millisecond, 30*time.Second, 2*time.Minute, 2.0, 1.0, clock)\n```\n\nThis ramps from 800ms up to a 30\u201360s ceiling, reducing API server load by ~98% during outages.\n\nWatch requests carry a random timeout in `[minWatchTimeout, 2*minWatchTimeout]` (default `[5m, 10m]`) to spread reconnect load across a cluster's many informers.\n\n### 4.2 DeltaFIFO Deduplication Semantics\n\n`DeltaFIFO` accumulates per-key delta slices rather than replacing them. This means:\n- If an object is Added, Updated, and Deleted before the consumer processes it, all three deltas survive in order.\n- Double-deletes collapse: appending a `Deleted` delta when the accumulator already ends with `Deleted` is a no-op (unless the existing terminal is a `DeletedFinalStateUnknown`, which gets replaced to preserve the most accurate last state).\n\nThis is a deliberate tradeoff: no change is lost, but memory grows if the consumer is slow. The consumer (`controller.processLoop`) holds the DeltaFIFO lock for the duration of processing each key's deltas, preventing concurrent modification from the Reflector.\n\n### 4.3 Slow Handler Backpressure\n\nThe `SharedInformer` contract (`shared_informer.go:130`) is explicit: **\"A client must process each notification promptly; a SharedInformer is not engineered to deal well with a large backlog of notifications.\"** Each `processorListener` has an internal ring buffer (`initialBufferSize = 1024`, `shared_informer.go:340`) with a separate `pop` goroutine that bridges `addCh` to `nextCh`. If the `run` goroutine (calling user code) is slow, the ring buffer grows unboundedly.\n\nThis is why the canonical pattern is for event handlers to only enqueue a key to a work queue, never to reconcile inline. The work queue provides the backpressure and retry semantics that the informer deliberately lacks.\n\n### 4.4 Late-Joining Handlers\n\nAdding an event handler to an already-running informer is safe but requires a protocol (`shared_informer.go:693`):\n1. Acquire `blockDeltas` mutex \u2014 stops all in-progress event distribution.\n2. Do a full scan of `indexer.List()` to send synthetic `Add` events for all known objects.\n3. Release mutex.\n\nThis ensures the new handler's view is consistent with the shared cache at join time, without restarting the informer.\n\n### 4.5 Client-Side Rate Limiting\n\nThe default `rest.Config` uses `QPS=5.0`, `Burst=10` (`rest/config.go:47`). These are intentionally conservative. In informer-based controllers, most reads hit the local cache (zero API calls), so the rate limit mainly governs write mutations. However, controllers that fan out to many resources (e.g., the deployment controller managing thousands of pods) can easily saturate these defaults. Operators should tune `QPS` and `Burst` based on actual write rates, and use per-resource clientsets with different configs when appropriate.\n\n### 4.6 Leader Election Tradeoffs\n\n`LeaderElector` (`leaderelection.go:116`) uses three configuration parameters:\n- `LeaseDuration` (default 15s): how long a non-leader waits before stealing a lease.\n- `RenewDeadline` (default 10s): how long the leader retries before giving up.\n- `RetryPeriod` (default 2s): polling interval.\n\nThe package comment (`leaderelection.go:28`) documents an important limitation: this implementation does **not** guarantee mutual exclusion in the presence of arbitrary clock skew or network partitions. `ReleaseOnCancel=true` creates a TOCTOU window \u2014 between the leader detecting context cancellation and the Lease being released, a new leader can be elected. This is by design for availability, not correctness. For strict fencing, an external mechanism is required.\n\n---\n\n## 5. Extension Points, Customization Hooks, and Associated Risks\n\n### 5.1 Transport Wrapping (`rest.Config.WrapTransport`)\n\n`rest.Config.WrapTransport` (`rest/config.go:115`) is the primary hook for injecting per-request middleware into the HTTP stack. It is called after the base TLS transport is constructed and receives the existing `http.RoundTripper`. Common uses: adding custom request headers, tracing instrumentation, logging.\n\n**Risk:** Panicking or blocking in a `RoundTripper` will hang or crash all operations through that client. Middleware must propagate context cancellation correctly (`req.Context()`) and must be goroutine-safe.\n\n### 5.2 `SharedInformer.SetTransform(TransformFunc)`\n\n`TransformFunc` (`shared_informer.go:231`) is called on each object before it enters the `Indexer`. Typical use: stripping unused fields (e.g., `managedFields`, large annotation blobs) to reduce cache memory footprint.\n\n**Risk:** Must be set **before** `Run()` is called; attempting to set it after start returns an error. The transform function must be idempotent and must not retain references to the passed object (mutation is forbidden).\n\n### 5.3 `SharedInformerFactory.WithTweakListOptions`\n\n`WithTweakListOptions` (`informers/factory.go:87`) allows injecting a `TweakListOptionsFunc` that modifies every `ListOptions` before a list or watch is issued. This is the hook for field selectors and label selectors at the factory level.\n\n**Risk:** Field selectors are evaluated on the API server, not the cache. If you filter at the factory level, the Indexer only sees the filtered subset. Controller code that relies on full cluster visibility will have blind spots.\n\n### 5.4 Custom `ResourceEventHandler`\n\nRegistering handlers via `AddEventHandler` or `AddEventHandlerWithResyncPeriod` is the primary extension point for controllers. `ResourceEventHandlerFuncs` (`controller.go:277`) is a convenience adapter that allows implementing only a subset of `OnAdd`/`OnUpdate`/`OnDelete`.\n\n**Risk:** Handlers are called **synchronously** from the `processorListener.run()` goroutine, under no informer lock but with the constraint that they must not block. Handlers that perform synchronous API calls or hold locks that conflict with informer internals risk deadlock or cascade delays across all listeners of the same informer.\n\n### 5.5 Custom `WatchErrorHandler`\n\n`SetWatchErrorHandlerWithContext` (`shared_informer.go:220`) replaces the default error logger. Useful for feeding watch error metrics into custom monitoring systems.\n\n**Risk:** Must be set before `Run()`. The handler must return quickly \u2014 any expensive processing blocks the Reflector's reconnect cycle.\n\n### 5.6 Custom Indexers\n\n`SharedIndexInformer.AddIndexers(Indexers)` (`shared_informer.go:269`) adds secondary indexes to the Indexer. An `IndexFunc` takes an object and returns a slice of indexed values (strings). This allows efficient lookups like \"all Pods with owner reference X\" without scanning the full cache.\n\n**Risk:** Indexes must be added before `Run()`. Adding indexes to a started informer is forbidden. Index functions must be pure (no side effects) and fast; they are called on every cache mutation.\n\n### 5.7 `applyconfigurations` and Server-Side Apply\n\nThe `applyconfigurations/` package provides type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply (SSA) payloads. SSA is architecturally distinct from the traditional `get-modify-update` loop: the client declares intent declaratively, and the server merges field ownership. This allows multiple controllers to manage different fields of the same object without stomping on each other.\n\n**Risk:** SSA patches must specify a `fieldManager` identity. Callers that switch between SSA and imperative updates on the same fields can create conflict errors.\n\n---\n\n## 6. Operational Implications\n\n### 6.1 Memory Footprint of the Informer Cache\n\nEach `SharedIndexInformer` maintains a full in-memory copy of every object matching its LIST/WATCH selector. In large clusters with thousands of Pods or Events, this can amount to hundreds of MiB per controller process. The `SetTransform` hook exists specifically to address this:\n\n```go\nfactory.WithTransform(func(obj interface{}) (interface{}, error) {\n    if pod, ok := obj.(*v1.Pod); ok {\n        pod.ManagedFields = nil\n        pod.Annotations = nil // if not needed by controller logic\n    }\n    return obj, nil\n})\n```\n\nThe `sharedInformerFactory` (`informers/factory.go:57`) propagates the transform to all informers it creates via `InformerFor()`:\n\n```go\n// informers/factory.go:216\ninformer.SetTransform(f.transform)\n```\n\n### 6.2 SharedInformerFactory and Informer Deduplication\n\nThe `sharedInformerFactory` (`informers/factory.go:66`) uses `reflect.Type` as the key for its `informers` map. This means that for a given process, there is exactly one `SharedIndexInformer` per object type (per factory). Multiple controllers that watch the same type share one watch stream, one cache, and one set of event buffers \u2014 dramatically reducing API server load compared to each controller maintaining its own watch.\n\nThe `Start(stopCh)` \u2192 `WaitForCacheSync(stopCh)` sequence is the idiomatic initialization:\n\n```go\n// factory.go:144\nfunc (f *sharedInformerFactory) Start(stopCh <-chan struct{}) {\n    for informerType, informer := range f.informers {\n        if !f.startedInformers[informerType] {\n            f.wg.Add(1)\n            go func() {\n                defer f.wg.Done()\n                informer.Run(stopCh)\n            }()\n        }\n    }\n}\n```\n\nCalling `Start()` after adding informers acquired post-first-start is safe; the method is idempotent per-type.\n\n### 6.3 Work Queue Backpressure and Retry Policy\n\n`DefaultTypedControllerRateLimiter` (`workqueue/default_rate_limiters.go:50`) composes two limiters with `MaxOfRateLimiter`:\n- **Per-item exponential backoff**: 5ms base, 1000s ceiling (`baseDelay*2^failures`).\n- **Global token bucket**: 10 QPS, burst 100.\n\nThe effective wait for a repeatedly failing item is `max(per-item-backoff, global-bucket-wait)`. The global bucket prevents a pathological item from starving healthy items via starvation of the global limiter.\n\nWork queues also deduplicate: `Add(key)` is a no-op if `key` is already in the queue or currently being processed. This means that a flood of informer events for the same object coalesces into a single reconciliation invocation, which is the intended behavior for level-based controllers.\n\n### 6.4 Feature Gates Affecting Informer Behavior\n\n`client-go` exposes its own feature gate registry (`staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/features/`):\n\n- **`WatchListClient`**: When enabled (`reflector.go:300`), the Reflector prefers the streaming `WatchList` API (KEP-3157), which reduces API server memory usage for large collections by streaming objects directly rather than building a full paginated list response.\n- **`InOrderInformersBatchProcess`**: Enables batch processing of Deltas from `DeltaFIFO`, potentially improving throughput.\n- **`ClientsAllowCBOR`**: Enables CBOR serialization as an alternative to JSON/Protobuf (`rest/config.go:715`).\n\nFeature gates affect behavior at the library level; consumers must ensure their API server supports the corresponding server-side features before enabling them.\n\n### 6.5 Reflector Metrics and Observability\n\n`reflector_metrics.go` registers Prometheus-compatible metrics through a `MetricsProvider` interface (the default provider is a no-op). Operators can register a real metrics provider to expose:\n- `reflector_lists_total` / `reflector_watches_total` \u2014 watch restarts signal API server instability.\n- `reflector_last_resource_version` \u2014 used to detect a stuck reflector.\n- `reflector_watch_duration_seconds` \u2014 watch stream health.\n\nThe work queue similarly exposes queue depth, processing latency, and retry counts through `workqueue/metrics.go`.\n\n---\n\n## 7. File and Directory Map\n\n```\nstaging/src/k8s.io/client-go/\n\u251c\u2500\u2500 ARCHITECTURE.md                         # Official architecture overview\n\u251c\u2500\u2500 rest/\n\u2502   \u251c\u2500\u2500 config.go                           # rest.Config, InClusterConfig, RESTClientFor\n\u2502   \u251c\u2500\u2500 client.go                           # RESTClient, builder pattern entry point\n\u2502   \u251c\u2500\u2500 request.go                          # Request struct, Do/Watch/Stream execution\n\u2502   \u251c\u2500\u2500 with_retry.go                       # WithRetry interface, withRetry impl, Retry-After handling\n\u2502   \u251c\u2500\u2500 transport.go                        # DefaultTypedControllerRateLimiter, exponential + bucket\n\u2502   \u251c\u2500\u2500 flowcontrol/                        # Token bucket (RateLimiter) for REST client QPS\n\u2502   \u2514\u2500\u2500 retry/                              # client-go-scoped feature gates (WatchListClient, etc.)\n```\n\n---\n\n## Summary\n\n`client-go`'s architecture is layered and deliberately decoupled:\n\n1. **Configuration** (`rest.Config`, `clientcmd`) is separated from **execution** (`RESTClient`, `Request`).\n2. **Authentication** is injected at transport construction time via `RoundTripper` chains, not at call sites.\n3. The **informer subsystem** replaces per-reconciliation API calls with a shared, eventually-consistent local cache. The DeltaFIFO queue ensures no event is lost, while the shared processor fan-out ensures all registered handlers receive notifications sequentially and independently.\n4. The **work queue** is the architectural seam between event detection (informer) and reconciliation (controller). Its deduplication, deferred execution, and rate-limited retry semantics are what make Kubernetes controllers robust to transient failures without starvation or tight loops.\n5. **Extension** is designed around composition: `WrapTransport` for HTTP middleware, `TransformFunc` for memory optimization, `AddIndexers` for efficient cache queries, and `ResourceEventHandler` for controller logic hooks \u2014 each with clear pre-conditions (most require configuration before `Run()`) and failure modes (slow handlers, panicking middleware) that must be understood by operators.\n",
